♠ Peak Date ♣ 309-379 CE ♥ Long reign of Shapur II (309-379 CE). Peace and security within empire. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_2_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]'Secular' or king's power was probably at its height in the early fifth century.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "first synod of the Nestorian Church was convened in 410" during reign of Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_3_IrSasn1] "Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon; he and the Jewish exilarch became responsible for their coreligionists."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_4_IrSasn1]
♠ Duration ♣ 205-487 CE ♥_Sasanid Period 1_ 205-487 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Conquest from 205 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The Sasanian campaign to control the province of Persis/Fars had begun in 205-6, when the father of Ardashir I, Pabag, had dethroned the local ruler of the city of Istakhr, the capital of Fars, by the name of Gozihr."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_5_IrSasn1] Later sources claimed Pabag was a priest at a fire-temple in Istakhr.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_6_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "King Papak, who usurped the crown of the Pars rulers, played a major role in unifying the land. He apparently had to wage a difficult struggle against the central Parthian government."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_7_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Empire from 226 CE (with God-king and Achaemenid ideology)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] King Papak's adopted son Ardashir inherited the crown. He was from the family of Sasan.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_8_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] The first Sasanian 'King of Kings' was Ardashir I who was crowned in 226 CE at Ctesiphon.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_9_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Early Sasanids in their imperial ideology "considered themselves from the lineage of the gods" and used the Achaemenid title "King of Kings."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_10_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Size of court and bureaucracy increases between Ardashir I and Shapur I (240-270 CE). Military success under Shapur I (240-270 CE) and Shapur II (309-379 CE). [MAJIDBENAM_REF_11_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Rise of Zoroastrian Church under Kerdir 274 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_12_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Zoroastrian priest Kerdir "began the persecution of the religious minorities in the empire, such as the Jews, Christians, Manichaeans, Mandeans, and Buddhists. ... Mani ... was imprisoned and put to death in 276 with the blessing (and to the relief) of Kerdir."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_13_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Status quo from 294-325 CE (Zoroastrian control)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] presumably the situation is the same under kings Narseh (293-303 CE) and Hormizd II (303-309 CE) and during the infancy of Shapur II when "the court and the Zoroastrian priests ran an empire that was secure and stable enough structurally and administratively to survive without a strong monarchy"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_14_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Long reign of Shapur II and rise of court/bureaucracy[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Under Shapur II, power of the nobility and priests increased substantially.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_15_IrSasn1] Does this imply at some point following the church of Kerdir and his persecutions the influence of priests diminished - perhaps due to the rise of the bureaucracy/court which may have accellerated during the infancy of Shapur II?[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Time of Shapur II has been referred to as a golden age.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Violence begins from 379 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] An inscription relates that Ardashar II (379-383 CE) purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_16_IrSasn1] The sophisticated, centralised bureaucracy was now "under the control of the priests" and its chief priest, with Kingship relegated to the status of a secular institution.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_17_IrSasn1] Ardashar may have purged a court/bureaucracy which had become over-mighty during the long (70-year) reign of Shapur II. This would have favoured the Zoroastrian priests.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] The kings that followed Ardashar II (379-383 CE) "all met a violent end."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_18_IrSasn1] that appears to mean up to 420 CE: Shapur III, Wahram IV, Yazdgird I, Shapur IV, Khosrau the Usurper (?). this elite conflict reflects a power-struggle between the court/bureaucracy and the Zoroastrian church.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]recognition of Nestorian Christianity 410 CE; ends with usurper 420 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE) called "the sinful one"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_19_IrSasn1] by Zoroastrian literature because he went against the wishes of the Zoroastrian priests.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] the 'secular' kings become powerful enough to challenge the priests. Under Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE) Christianity was officially recognized.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_20_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "first synod of the Nestorian Church was convened in 410" during reign of Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_21_IrSasn1] "Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon; he and the Jewish exilarch became responsible for their coreligionists."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_22_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Persecution of Christians and Jews from c.420 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Bahram V (420-438 CE) and Yazdgird II (438-457 CE) persecuted Christians
"Bahram V continued and intensified the persecution of Yazdagird's last days." Forced conversions. Property confiscated. Churches destroyed. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_23_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Yazdgird II (438-457 CE) is noted for his persecution of Christians and Jews.
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Infighting from 457 CE, famine and Hephthalites[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Hormizd III (457-459 CE) defeated in battle by Peroz (459-484 CE) who was aided by Hephthalites (?)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Seven-year famine (464-471)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] War with Kidarites and Hephthalites[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Peroz captured by Hephthalites[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Balash (484-488 CE) was deposed by nobility and priests.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_24_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] The first reign of Kavad I (488-496 CE) was ended by "dissatisfied nobility and priests" who had him imprisoned.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_25_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]
_Sasanid Period 3_ 488-642 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Reforms during the long reigns of Kavad I and Khusrau I[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Kavad I (499-531 CE) 21. Khusrau I (531-579 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Khusrau I (531-579 CE) promoted minor nobility and reduced the power of aristocrats and their estates. Deghans became tax collectors. "For the first time, the power of the landed nobility was restricted and all the taxes were in the hands of the king."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_26_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Khusrau I is credited with wise leadership and is known as "Plato's philosopher king."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_27_IrSasn1] In 570s CE Sasanian Empire was "at the apex of its glory and power, headed by a philosopher king" (Khosrau I).[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Instablity from 579 CE[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Hamizid IV (579-590 CE), who followed Khosrau I, had many enemies at court, killed many of the nobility and was harsh to the priests.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Hormizd IV deposed 589-590 CE by general and nobility who put on the throne his son, Khusrau II.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_28_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Khusrau II forced to flee to Byzantium for the years 590-591 CE by Bahram but recruited an Armenenian army to regain the throne.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_29_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Kushrau II was deposed by nobility and priests in 628 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_30_IrSasn1] Khosrau II (590-628 CE) was forced to seek shelter in Byzantine Hierapolis against a challenger king, Wahram Chubin, who minted coins 590-591 CE. Khosrau II regained the throne (purges?) and then the empire reached its greatest territorial extent. Khosrau II was deposed by priests and nobility in 628 CE.[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Kavad II (628-630 CE) conducted a fratricide, killing all the male heirs in the Sasanid family, and was assassinated.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_31_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] By 630s CE the empire was in confusion, had disintegrated into regional power-bases and internal conflict when Khuzistan fell to Caliph Umar. Arabs conquered the Sasanid stronghold (Persis) in 650 CE.

♠ Capital ♣ Ctesiphon; Dastagird; Ecbatana ♥ Ctesiphon: 270-499 CE First two rulers based in Persis homeland. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_36_IrSasn1] Ctesiphon was the administrative capital, seat of the king and the most important for economic and strategic reasons.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_37_IrSasn1] Ecbatana was a summer capital.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_38_IrSasn1] Istakhr in Fars was an administrative, religious and economic centre that also was "the ideological heart of the empire, since the temple of the dynasty's fire - the coronation place of many Sasanian rulers - was situated there."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_39_IrSasn1] Dastagird was a capital during the reign of Khurau II.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_40_IrSasn1]
♠ Language ♣ Pahlavi ♥ "The most widespread languages during the Sasanian era were Middle Persian (or Pahlavi), Parthian, Sogdian, Khwarizmian, Khotanese Saka and Bactrian; various texts in these languages are extant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_41_IrSasn1] In Iraq: "Parsi-speaking Zoroastrians ruled Iraq, but the local populations were Aramaic-speaking Nestorians and Jews" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_42_IrSasn1] "Although we know little of Parthian administrative practice we may assume it provided the basis for the Sasanian administration that followed it in a closely related form of Middle Persian, and in a related script, in the 3rd century AD."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_43_IrSasn1]♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European ♥

♠ Alternate Religion ♣ ♥http://www.ancient.eu.com/uploads/images/1190.gif[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The Sassanids came from the Parthian province of Persis near the Zagros mountains of western Iran. Ardashir I defeated the last Parthian ruler Ardawan (Artabanus IV) in 224 CE and claimed the title "King of Kings" in imitation of the ancient Archaemenids. The early Sassanid rulers claimed a divine descent until powerful Zoroastrian priests ended this by the 4th century. The long reign of Shapur II (309-379 CE) saw the peak of peace and security within the empire[MAJIDBENAM_REF_44_IrSasn1] that had a total population of perhaps 5 million people.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]In the early period royal cities were built and administered by shahrabs who ruled as petty kings.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_45_IrSasn1] Centralization occurred in the later Sassanid period when the empire was split into four parts each ruled by a spahbad who had civil and military powers.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_46_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The size of Persian court and bureaucracy notability increases between Ardashir I and Shapur I (240-270 CE).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_47_IrSasn1] Institutions of administration continued to evolve from those present in the Parthian era[MAJIDBENAM_REF_48_IrSasn1] a grand vizier now present at the central government in the capital Ctesiphon whose remit encompassed control of the diwans, diplomatic affairs as well as occasional stints as commander of the army.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_49_IrSasn1] By the fifth century the centralized bureaucracy was sophisticated enough that "the death of a king would not bring the empire down."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_50_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Rise of Zoroastrian Church under Kerdir 274 CE had monumental impact on Persia with the persecution of religious minorities (Christians, Manichaeans, Mandeans, Jews and Buddhists)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_51_IrSasn1]. The Zoroastrian priests had initially tolerated rival religious such as Manichaeism until Shapur I (240-270 CE) but Mani was eventually executed.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_52_IrSasn1] By the time of Bahram II (274-293 CE) the Sasanian kings themselves had lost their own religious freedom as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to a priest called Kerdir "who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_53_IrSasn1]

♠ Administrative levels ♣ 6 ♥Early Sassanid period[MAJIDBENAM_BR] administration in provinces and districts "did not differ greatly from that under the Parthians."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_61_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] In the early period "royal cities, almost equivalent to semi-independent kingdoms, were built" administered by a shahrab.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_62_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Centralization occurred in the later Sassanid period when the empire was split into four parts each ruled by a spahbad who had civil and military powers.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_63_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]

Administration based in Ctesiphon[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_65_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. rulers called shahrabs, appointed by King of Kings[MAJIDBENAM_REF_70_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "royal" capital cities[MAJIDBENAM_REF_71_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] military garrison[MAJIDBENAM_REF_72_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] The ruler of Armenia had a special title: "Great King of Armenia." ♠ Religious levels ♣ [4-5] ♥_Zoroastrianism_ "or more exactly Mazdaism"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_78_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Third-century CE Zoroastrian two priests were highly influential in the development of Zoroastrianism as the Sasanid state religion (three if we include Pabag, the founder of the Sasanian dynasty, who was a priest).[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Kerdir "may be considered the father of the Zoroastrian church in this period, as he was the one who attempted to make Zoroastrianism into a uniform body, with a unified doctrine, attached to the state."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_79_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The Sasanian sources state that Tosar was responsible for the codification of the Avesta ... Kerdir brought about the organization of the church and a religious hierarchy."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_80_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]
1. King of Kings (until Shapur II)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Ohrmaz mowbed (chief priest)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_81_IrSasn1] mowbedan mowbed[MAJIDBENAM_REF_82_IrSasn1]
mowbedan mowbed was the "head of the religious order"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_83_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Kerdir was one powerful Zoroastrian priest, caretaker of the Anahid fire temple at time of Wahram II (274-293 CE).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_84_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "When Sassanid kings were raised to the throne they received the insignia of royal authority from the chief Mobedh who held the highest religious office."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_85_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. mowbed (district level) (head priest)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_86_IrSasn1]
"important functions and carried out legal as well as religious and administrative duties.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_87_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4. mow/mog
The magus (mow/mog) had a higher status and later was also involved in economic and legal matters. Above him was the chief magus (mowbed), who held an important position and was probably the main religious authority throughout the empire."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_88_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4-5. herbeds (teacher priests)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_89_IrSasn1]
"instructed the people in daily ritual, prayer, and tradition and tended the fire."[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Three major fire-temples were established for the three classes ... Smaller fire-temples existed in the villages and towns, attended by a teacher-priest (herbed)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_90_IrSasn1]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Magians had a hierarchy parallel to that of the state, a hierarchical judicial administration specifically for Zoroastrians, a cult, scriptures, religious laws, and distinctive customs. It was the religion of the elite and rulers."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_91_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Eight different priests required for some Zoroastrian rituals e.g. vispered ritual and the videvad sade purification ritual "who took up specific positions in the ritual area, also described in the Nirangestan."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_92_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] haoma-pressing priest (hawanan)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] fire-lighting priest (atr-wakhsh)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] presenting priest (frabertar)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] tending priest, who brings water (abert or danu-uzwaza, which refers to the river Danu)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] washing priest (asnatar)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] mingling priest (raethwish-kar)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] auditing priest (sraoshawarz)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] one who brings sacrificial animal (pasu-wazah)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Comprehensive source on Zoroastrian religion: Moazami (2016) "Zoroastrianism: Religious texts, theology, history and culture."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_93_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]_Nestorian Christianity_[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"first synod of the Nestorian Church was convened in 410" during reign of Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_94_IrSasn1] "Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon; he and the Jewish exilarch became responsible for their coreligionists."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_95_IrSasn1] "The Sasanian state used the churches as intermediaries to regulate and tax the population."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_96_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. Patriach[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Persian Christianity became officially recognized and the Nestorian Patriach resided at the royal city of Ctesiphon".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_97_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] the Sasanid king "organized a Christian Persian church that grew in number, and many in the royal family and the nobility, especially the women, gravitated toward this religion."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_98_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Catholicos in province
"The Christian community was headed by the Catholicos"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_99_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "The Catholicos in each province oversaw the Christian congregation and provided money and guidance for the community."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_100_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 3. Metropolitan
"The Sasanians appointed a catholicos or patriarch and a metropolitan to preside over the bishops in parallel with the Sasanian administrative hierarchy." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_101_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 4. Bishops of Bishoprics
"According to al-Biruni, Christianity had reached Merv within 200 years of the birth of Christ and the first reference to a Merv bishopric dates to the year 334."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_102_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 5. Heads of Churches
"by the end of the Sasanian period there were churches and bishoprics established throughout the empire, and many from the royal family also converted to Christianity."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_103_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Royal permission was required for the election of the heads of churches, for construction of buildings, for burials, and even for the issue of monastic rules."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_104_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] 6.

♠ Military levels ♣ [7-9] ♥Like the Achaemenids, the Sasanids likely used the decimal system to organize the Spah (army). The title Hazarmard/Hazarbad means "chief of a thousand." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_110_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Khusrau I (later Sassanid period) changed the command structure. "Previously the entire army had been under the command of an officer known as the spahbad. Now, four commanders were appointed, each in charge of the troops of one-quarter of the country. Each of these newly created commanders had a deputy called a marzban."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_111_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. King[MAJIDBENAM_BR] 2. Great commander (Vuzurg-Framander. Managed state affairs whilst monarch on military expedition).

4. Spah (lead by a Spahbad, army general)

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥ Merchant commerce in the bazaar, a profession list: Blacksmith; Iron-molder; silversmith; silver-molder; roof maker; string maker; mortar workers (possibly spices); ironsmith; tailor; dressmaker; porclain; potmaker; carpenter; washerman; shoemaker; shoemaker of shoes with strings; potter; baker; book painter; general painter; cup-maker; tanner; dyer; builders; barber; tent-maker; sweetmakers; tablecloth makers; goldsmith; sadler. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_113_IrSasn1]
♠ Professional military officers ♣ absent ♥ Sasanid society had four classes: warriors, scribes, priests, and commoners. The warriors (Arteshtaran) were an hereditary elite.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_114_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Seven aristocratic families dominated the military and government leadership positions. All except the Sassans were Parthian in origin. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_115_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] House of Sassan, Aspahbad-Pahlav (Gurgan), Karin-Pahlav (Shiraz), Suren-Pahlav (Seistan), Spandiyadh (Nihavand), Mihram (Rayy), Guiw[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Before the reforms of Khusrau I (later Sassanid period) "all nobles, great and small, had been obliged to equip themselves and their followers and serve in the army without pay, but Khusrau issued equipment to the poorer nobles and paid a salary for their services. Consequently, the power of the great nobles - who frequently had their own private armies - was reduced."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_116_IrSasn1]
♠ Professional soldiers ♣ absent ♥ "Payment for service might have arisen because of the vast income from silver mines, among other sources." Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ disputed_absent_present ♥absent[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "codified law did not exist in Sasanian Iran" the Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions "cannot be considered a legal code. It is one of the collections that were compiled as manuals for the administration of justice."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_122_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Codification of Sasanian law occurred Khusrau I - Khusrau II. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_123_IrSasn1] later Sasanid period[MAJIDBENAM_BR]present[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "In the specialist literature, the Madigan has become known as the 'Sasanian Legal Code'. ... It is possible to reconstitute practically the entire system of Iranian law on the basis of the mass of information contained in the Code."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_124_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] According to the Dankard, a judge had to consider the Avesta, its Pahlavi translation and commentaries, and "the consesus of the Righteous (ham-dadestanith i wehan)".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_125_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] The law was based on religion, specifically "the Holy Scripture of the Avesta and its translation and commentaries in Pahlavi".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_126_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Court cases judged on Zoroastrian law, unless both parties from another religion. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_127_IrSasn1] Codification of Sasanian law occurred Khusrau I - Khusrau II c.531 CE. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_128_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Link between Iranian law and Zoroastrian religion shown in Madigan-i hazar dadestan [Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions] c620 CE, author "was a contemporary of Khusrau II."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_129_IrSasn1]
♠ Judges ♣ present ♥The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[MAJIDBENAM_REF_130_IrSasn1], whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_131_IrSasn1] is unknown. The mowbed were priest judges.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_132_IrSasn1] Judges known as rads were among other city officials including tax officials who "represented the central government and were responsible to provincial administrators".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_133_IrSasn1] The king could "pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_134_IrSasn1]
♠ Courts ♣ inferred present ♥"Not obeying an order to appear in court was regarded as an obstruction of justice (azismand)".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_135_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[MAJIDBENAM_REF_136_IrSasn1], whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_137_IrSasn1] is unknown. The descriptions of early travellers and historians, and the evidence of archaeology, agree in ascribing to the Sassanid monarchy and nobility a dazzling richness of architectural decoration, costume, and jewelery." Information[edit]

♠ Length ♣ inferred present ♥ Bivar (2010) says: "The principal units of length in the Near Eastern measurement systems were the foot and the cubit, standing in the ratio of 1:1.5. Subdivisions of the foot were its quarter, the palm, and its 16th, the finger. A higher unit, the fathom, consisted of four cubits."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_172_IrSasn1]
♠ Area ♣ inferred present ♥
♠ Volume ♣ inferred present ♥ Bivar (2010) says there was an attempt at harmonization under the Achaemenids. "The dominant units of capacity in the Achaemenid period are defined by a vessel recovered in the excavations at Persepolis. As in the case of weight-units, the reforms of Darius seem to have made an attempt to harmonize the Babylonian and Old Iranian systems."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_173_IrSasn1] Devices, called water distributors, were used to "measure and calibrate the flow" of water in rivers." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_174_IrSasn1]
♠ Weight ♣ inferred present ♥ Bivar (2010) says there was a universal standard under the Achaemenids: the standard unit of weight of the shekel "was enforced throughout the Archaemenid Empire by Darius the Great ... around 515 BCE. In this system, the shekel stood at 8.40 grams."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_175_IrSasn1]

♠ History ♣ present ♥ "Important events of the reign of each of the Sasanian kings were written down and preserved in the imperial archives, a practice that probably dates from the very beginning of Sasanian rule."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_185_IrSasn1] "in about the 5th century, priests attached to the Sassanid court began to compile an immense chronicle, the Khwaday Namag" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_186_IrSasn1] - however the "Book of Kings" might also be considered fiction, but to the extent it is a pretense at a history it is historical writing. "The idea of compiling a written national history for the Iranians appeared toward the end of the Sasanian period, especially at the time of Khusrau I, during whose reign books were either written in Pahlavi or translated from other languages, such as Syriac, the Indian languages and Greek."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_187_IrSasn1]
♠ Philosophy ♣ inferred present ♥ "Iranians were familiar with Greek philosophy from the Achaemenid period. This acquaintance was deepened in Sasanian times, leading to the influence of Greek philosophy on Zoroastrian religious works."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_188_IrSasn1] Advice to kings genre: "Several works discussed government policies and ways and means of governing the kingdom. Among them is the Name-i Tansar [Letter of Tansar], written by Tansar (or, in the correct form, Tosar), the Zoroastrian mobad (high priest) at the time of Ardashir I, in response to Gushnasp, king of Tabaristan. ... changes were made to it in later periods, particularly during the reign of Khusrau I.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_189_IrSasn1]
♠ Scientific literature ♣ inferred present ♥ "Iranian interest in Indian philosophy and science during the Sasanian period is demonstrated by translations into Middle Persian of Indian works on mathematics, astronomy and medicine, and of belles-lettres and didactic texts".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_190_IrSasn1] in volume more in later Sassanid period than the first Court doctors, including Indian doctors, suggest Indian medical works likely translated.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_191_IrSasn1] However, most scientific literature translated was from Syriac and Greek.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_192_IrSasn1] "astrologers and the various kinds of medical specialists".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_193_IrSasn1]
♠ Fiction ♣ inferred present ♥ "The interest in oral literature in pre-Islamic Iran meant that, apart from state or commerical records and documents and, on rare occasions, religious works, nothing was written down until the Sasanian period. Secular oral literature was preserved orally by gosan (poet-ministrels) or khunyagar (story-tellers)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_194_IrSasn1] Religious and secular writings but secular writings "written within the framework of Zoroastrian religious beliefs".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_195_IrSasn1] "Epic stories, frequently in verse, remained an oral form until the Sasanian period and some were used in the compilation of the Khwaday-namag [Book of Lords] ... in Pahlavi."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_196_IrSasn1] must have been written fiction of Greek works or derived from Greek works, even if only read by Greeks themselves, in the cities, which may still have had Greek communities. on the basis of Persian tradition only though the code would appear to be inferred absent at this time.

There were way stations where the couriers could rest and where fresh horses could be obtained. ... Under the Sasanians a similar postal system appears to have been in operation; in a peace treaty concluded with Byzantium in a.d. 561 one clause stipulated that envoys should be supplied with mounts at the postal stations maintained by both empires."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_210_IrSasn1] The barid of the Islamic era thought to have been based on earlier system of postal stations.

Military Technologies[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ inferred present ♥ bronze is made with copper. Copper weapons present in preceding Parthian polity [MAJIDBENAM_REF_211_IrSasn1]

♠ Steel ♣ present ♥ "It is believed that Indian steel was exported in the early centuries A.D. and was known even in the time of Alexander. By the sixth century there is more definite evidence of the manufacture of Damascene swords and the steel used for this purpose came from India."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_214_IrSasn1] Artaxerxes II of Persia (Achaemenids, ruled around 400 BCE) had a Greek physician called Ctesias of Cnidus who was impressed by his sword of Indian steel.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_215_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_REF_216_IrSasn1] Use of Damascene steel certainly by 540 CE: "This unique type of steel was a major technological innovation and Iran played an important role in its production over the centuries. Circumstantial evidence suggests that a trade in a special steel, conceivably the ingots from which damascene steel was made, was underway in the Parthian and Sasanian period. Sometime after 115 A.D. the Parthians were importing iron (steel) from some point to the east" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_217_IrSasn1] "High-carbon steel was being produced in the eastern Iranian region from the tenth century CE."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_218_IrSasn1]

♠ Composite bow ♣ present ♥ Composite bow of Central Asian design, made out of horn, wood and sinew. Had a range of 175 meters, accurate within 50-60 meters.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_221_IrSasn1] at the muster parades of Khusrau I (second Sassanid period) cavalry units required to have "mail, breastplate, helmet, leg guards, arm guards, horse armour, lance, buckler, sword, mace, battle axe, quiver of thirty arrows, bow case with two bows, and two spare bow strings."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_222_IrSasn1]
♠ Crossbow ♣ inferred present ♥ "the Persian nawak, also known by its Arabic name of majra or mijrat. An early reference is the use of it by the Sassanid Persians against the Arabs in +637 when it was termed qaus al-nawakiyah (the tube bow). In the Islamic world extraordinary distances were shot with this device." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_223_IrSasn1] Present in preceding and succeeding polities.
♠ Tension siege engines ♣ present ♥ "Adapting Roman methods, Sassanid siege technology advanced greatly between the first and sixth centuries. The Sassanians employed offensive siege weapons such as scorpions, ballistae, battering rams, and moving towers."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_224_IrSasn1]
♠ Sling siege engines ♣ absent ♥First known use during Byzantine Empire.

The defences include the city’s northern and southern walls, the citadel and a wall strengthened by stone forts that stretched 40 km to the Caucasus mountains."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_264_IrSasn1]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ present ♥ The Sasanid Spah was a standing army. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_266_IrSasn1]
♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Controls were extremely stringent where industry dealt with state organisations such as the army, court and administration."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_267_IrSasn1] However, was this true only after the reforms of Khosrow?[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"From the time of Khosrow's reformation of the army, the king had provided the Sassanid "knights" with horses, equipment, and money, if they possessed no assets themselves. An accurate accounting was kept of weapons, pay, and the conditions of the horses; the "vassals'" armor was stored in the royal armories."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_268_IrSasn1]
♠ Total army ♣ [20,000-30,000] ♥ A "regular Sassanian army" numbered 12,000. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_269_IrSasn1] This number does not include infantry and siege workers?
♠ Professional army ♣ [20,000-30,000] ♥
♠ Largest fielded army ♣ 12,000 ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_270_IrSasn1]
♠ Maximum logistical lag ♣ ♥
♠ Sources of recruits ♣ domestic ♥Domestic forces made up the core of the Sasanian cavalry. In the earlier Sasanian Empire, Azadan nobility, descendants of ancient Aryan clans, dominated the elite Savaran unit. In the later period, the reforms of Khosrau I (531-579 CE) enabled lower nobility, the Dehkans, into the ranks of the Savaran.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_271_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"all nobles, great and small, had been obliged to equip themselves and their followers and serve in the army without pay, but Khusrau issued equipment to the poorer nobles and paid a salary for their services. Consequently, the power of the great nobles - who frequently had their own private armies - was reduced."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_272_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Infantry - peasants on military service. Cavalry - nobles, who before Khusrau I were unpaid and often had their own private armies.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_273_IrSasn1]
♠ Procurement of equipment and animals ♣ by soldiers ♥ Not until Khosrau I (531-579 CE) in the second period of the Sasanid Empire did the army receive horses and equipment from the state.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_274_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The ruler provided armor for his nobles with fiefs in addition to what they themselves supplied."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_275_IrSasn1]

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥ Palmyrans, Armenia, Huns, Roman Empire.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Parthians "annihilated" at Battle of Hormizdagan.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_276_IrSasn1]
♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥Intra-elite conflicts.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_277_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Religious persecution of Christians and Jews.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_278_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The kings that followed Ardashar II (379-383 CE) "all met a violent end."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_279_IrSasn1] that appears to mean up to 420 CE: Shapur III, Wahram IV, Yazdgird I, Shapur IV, Khosrau the Usurper (?). Jamasp (497-499 CE) put on throne by nobility whom had deposed Kavad I. Shapur III killed by nobles who cut ropes of a large tent so that it fell on him. Khosrau the Usurper 420 CE between Shapur IV (who was killed) Bahram V (420-338 CE) and Yazgird II (438-457 CE) do not appear to have net a violent end - the latter was very pro-Zoroastrian and persecuted Christians and Jews. Balash (484-488 CE) was deposed by nobility and priests.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_280_IrSasn1]
♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥ Balash (484-488 CE) was deposed by nobility and priests. The first reign of Kavad I (488-496 CE) was ended by "dissatisfied nobility and priests" who had him imprisoned.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_281_IrSasn1]
♠ Popular uprisings ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Military revolts ♣ present ♥ Hormizd IV deposed 589-590 CE by general and nobility who put on the throne his son, Khusrau II.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_282_IrSasn1]
♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ rural succession ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Violent civil conflict ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Violent riots urban ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Oppression / extermination of subaltern group ♣ present ♥ E.g. Zoroastrian priest Kerdir "began the persecution of the religious minorities in the empire, such as the Jews, Christians, Manichaeans, Mandeans, and Buddhists. ... Mani ... was imprisoned and put to death in 276 with the blessing (and to the relief) of Kerdir."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_283_IrSasn1]
♠ Wars within same ethnic group ♣ present ♥ The kings that followed Ardashar II (379-383 CE) "all met a violent end."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_284_IrSasn1]
♠ Wars involving groups with different dialects ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Wars involving groups with different languages ♣ present ♥ Previously coded "present" with the war against the Parthians as an example.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_285_IrSasn1] However, both polities spoke Pahlavi. Moreover, "Although we know little of Parthian administrative practice we may assume it provided the basis for the Sasanian administration that followed it in a closely related form of Middle Persian, and in a related script, in the 3rd century AD."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_286_IrSasn1] However, the Sasanians did fight wars against polities who spoke a different language from them, an example being the Romans, who spoke Latin. "In his invasion of Armenia, Syria and Cappadocia, Ardashir came into conflict with Rome and Emperor Alexander Severus (222–235 CE). In a letter to Ardashir, Alexander Severus had made it clear that his invasion of the Roman Empire would not be as successful as his conquest of his other neighbors. While Severus was alive, neither Ardashir nor the Romans were able to defeat one another (wars of 231–233 CE)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_287_IrSasn1]
♠ Wars involving groups with different language families ♣ present ♥ E.g. Arabs (Afro-Asiatic). "The Arabs in eastern Arabia raided the southwestern provinces of the Sasanian Empire, while Constantine and the other emperors battled for the soul of the Roman Empire which made the Persians safe from the Western front. When Shabuhr II came of age (325 CE), he took revenge on the Arabs and hence received the title “Shoulder Piercer” (Arabic Dhū al-Aktāf), referring to the punishment inflicted on the Arab tribes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_288_IrSasn1] "The most widespread languages during the Sasanian era were Middle Persian (or Pahlavi), Parthian, Sogdian, Khwarizmian, Khotanese Saka and Bactrian; various texts in these languages are extant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_289_IrSasn1] These are all Indo-European languages. Previously coded "inferred present" with the example of wars against the Huns, and the note that the classification of the Hunnic language remains unclear.
♠ Wars between different religious sects ♣ present ♥ "The Sassanid emperors' religious zeal fueled upheavals and ethnoreligious disputes in the empire and caused genocide and deportation of many tribes and ethnic groups."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_290_IrSasn1]
♠ Wars between different world religions ♣ present ♥ Zoroastrianism vs Christianity. Persecution of Christians and Jews from c420 CE. Bahram V (420-438 CE) and Yazdgird II (438-457 CE) persecuted Christians. "Bahram V continued and intensified the persecution of Yazdagird's last days." Forced conversions. Property confiscated. Churches destroyed.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_291_IrSasn1]
♠ Wars of world religion against tribal religion ♣ present ♥ E.g. Zoroastrianism vs. Turkic shamanism. "The religious beliefs of the Türk focused on a sky god, Tängri, and an earth goddess, Umay." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_292_IrSasn1]
♠ Wars on Urbanism-Literacy frontier ♣ present ♥ Huns. "A direct confrontation between the Huns and the Persian empire first occurred twenty years after the beginning of the great migration. In the summer of 395, hordes of Huns crossed the Don near its estuary, turned southeast, and made their way through the Caucasus into Persia and the Roman provinces. While the plundering of the Roman areas is variously attested (for sources, see Maenchen-Helfen, pp. 38-42), only Priscus (frag. 11) and the Liber Calipharum (Chronicon miscellaneum 3.4; tr., pp. 106-7) report the invasion of the Persian empire. Under the leadership of Basikh and Koursikh, a detachment of Huns rode down the valleys of the Euphrates and Tigris to Ctesiphon. Upon the news that a Persian army was marching towards them, the Huns turned back; but they were eventually caught. The Persians then managed to kill some of the Huns, to take almost their entire booty from them, and allegedly to free 18,000 prisoners. The rest of the Huns troops made their way back into the steppe over the Darband pass."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_293_IrSasn1]
♠ Wars on steppe frontier ♣ present ♥ Huns. "A direct confrontation between the Huns and the Persian empire first occurred twenty years after the beginning of the great migration. In the summer of 395, hordes of Huns crossed the Don near its estuary, turned southeast, and made their way through the Caucasus into Persia and the Roman provinces. While the plundering of the Roman areas is variously attested (for sources, see Maenchen-Helfen, pp. 38-42), only Priscus (frag. 11) and the Liber Calipharum (Chronicon miscellaneum 3.4; tr., pp. 106-7) report the invasion of the Persian empire. Under the leadership of Basikh and Koursikh, a detachment of Huns rode down the valleys of the Euphrates and Tigris to Ctesiphon. Upon the news that a Persian army was marching towards them, the Huns turned back; but they were eventually caught. The Persians then managed to kill some of the Huns, to take almost their entire booty from them, and allegedly to free 18,000 prisoners. The rest of the Huns troops made their way back into the steppe over the Darband pass."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_294_IrSasn1]
♠ Wars on agricultural frontier ♣ absent ♥
♠ Annexation ♣ present ♥ E.g. "Walakhash/Balash, the Parthian challenger to Ardawan IV in Mesopotamia, outlived the Parthian king, but he was the next victim of Ardashir in 229 CE. By this time most of the Iranian Plateau and the Arab side of the Persian Gulf had become part of his empire."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_295_IrSasn1]
♠ Looting ♣ present ♥ "In spite of the invasions of the Sasanian kings deep into Roman territory, and Roman military expeditions into the Persian heartland, it seems never to have been the intention of either power to occupy capture territory outside of north Mesopotamia permanently; many military encounters were basically wars of plunder."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_296_IrSasn1]
♠ Sack ♣ present ♥ Siege of Amida 359 CE. 73 days. Population deported to Khuzistan. City sacked.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_297_IrSasn1] Kushan city of Begram sacked by Shapur I.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_298_IrSasn1]
♠ Rape ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Deportation ♣ present ♥ Siege of Amida 359 CE. 73 days. Population deported to Khuzistan. City sacked.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_299_IrSasn1] "The Sassanid emperors' religious zeal fueled upheavals and ethnoreligious disputes in the empire and caused genocide and deportation of many tribes and ethnic groups."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_300_IrSasn1] 260 CE when Syria taken, Roman emperor Valerian, senators and soldiers were deported to Bishapur.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_301_IrSasn1] "[A]t the battle of Avarair in 451 CE the Armenian forces, led by Vardan of the Mamikonian’s family, were annihilated, and many were deported to Persia. This calamity was not to be forgotten by the Armenian (Christian) people and became a symbol of resistance and remembrance against their Zoroastrian neighbors."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_302_IrSasn1]
♠ Expulsion ♣ present ♥ "As a result of [Shabuhr II's] campaigns some of the Arabs were pushed into the heartland of Arabia and the Persian Gulf region remained in the hands of the Sasanian Empire."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_303_IrSasn1] Note also that in the following quote the word "deportation" may well be used to describe what we would refer to as "expulsion". "The Sassanid emperors' religious zeal fueled upheavals and ethnoreligious disputes in the empire and caused genocide and deportation of many tribes and ethnic groups."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_304_IrSasn1]
♠ Enslavement ♣ present ♥ "Slavery was more widespread in the Sasanid period. There were different categories of slave: bandag (servant); ansahrig (outlander); wardag (captive). The servant was usually in the household or a fire temple. The ansahrig usually worked on a farm and was non-Zoroastrian. A wardag worked on the land, in construction or other manual labor."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_305_IrSasn1] "[Emperor] Valerian never returned and died in Sasanian captivity, possibly in the city of Gundeshapur ('the weapons of Shapur'), which was built with the manpower of the thousands of Romans Shapur had taken prisoner."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_306_IrSasn1]
♠ Imprisonment ♣ present ♥ "[Emperor] Valerian never returned and died in Sasanian captivity, possibly in the city of Gundeshapur ('the weapons of Shapur'), which was built with the manpower of the thousands of Romans Shapur had taken prisoner."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_307_IrSasn1]
♠ Destruction ♣ present ♥ E.g. as part of conflict with Romans in 361-363 CE: "Julian had destroyed his own naval ships, so that his forces would not retreat, and Shabuhr II responded by adopting a scorched-earth policy in Mesopotamia which resulted in hunger among the Roman forces."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_308_IrSasn1] Also the persecution of Christians under Yazdagird included the destruction of churches.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_309_IrSasn1]
♠ Torture ♣ inferred present ♥ "Ordeal and torture were tools of the state, and were of great refinement and cruelty." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_310_IrSasn1] "The Arabs in eastern Arabia raided the southwestern provinces of the Sasanian Empire, while Constantine and the other emperors battled for the soul of the Roman Empire which made the Persians safe from the Western front. When Shabuhr II came of age (325 CE), he took revenge on the Arabs and hence received the title “Shoulder Piercer” (Arabic Dhū al-Aktāf), referring to the punishment inflicted on the Arab tribes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_311_IrSasn1]
♠ Mutilation ♣ inferred present ♥ "Ordeal and torture were tools of the state, and were of great refinement and cruelty."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_312_IrSasn1] "The Arabs in eastern Arabia raided the southwestern provinces of the Sasanian Empire, while Constantine and the other emperors battled for the soul of the Roman Empire which made the Persians safe from the Western front. When Shabuhr II came of age (325 CE), he took revenge on the Arabs and hence received the title “Shoulder Piercer” (Arabic Dhū al-Aktāf), referring to the punishment inflicted on the Arab tribes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_313_IrSasn1]
♠ Targeted massacre ♣ present ♥ "[M]assacre of the Jews of Isfahan."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_314_IrSasn1] Note that it is unclear what percentage of the population of Isfahan was killed, or what percentage of the Jewish population of Isfahan was killed."Reforms of Kavād and Ḵosrow I. Having seen the consequences of lawlessness and radical social practices, Kavād supported Zoroastrian orthodoxy, massacred the Mazdakite heretics, and subjugated the unruly nobility, a good many of whom had been killed, dispersed, or impoverished by the Mazdakite upheaval (Christensen, Iran Sass., pp. 357-58)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_315_IrSasn1]
♠ General massacre ♣ inferred present ♥ Previously coded "present" with the following example: "massacre of the Jews of Isfahan."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_316_IrSasn1] However, it is unclear what percentage of the population of Isfahan was killed, or what percentage of the Jewish population of Isfahan was killed.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Amida was eventually captured in 359 because Roman attempts to counter the Persian siege ramp eventually failed. By contrast, the city’s capture in January 503 was more anticlimactic: after three months of siege and with the onset of winter, the Amidenes apparently grew lax in their monitoring of enemy activities and failed to anticipate a simple Persian nighttime foray with ladders which allowed them to seize control of a section of the city walls. There were suspicions that treachery had played a part in 503, and a deserter in 359 who helped Persian archers gain temporary control of a tower one night almost succeeded in bringing that siege to an earlier end. Of course as the length of time the city was invested dragged on, so growing food shortages took an increasing toll on the physical stamina of soldiers and civilians, with disease also breaking out in 359—a reminder that sieges were the occasions in which warfare impinged most directly on the lives of noncombatants (Lee 2007: 133–8). That impact was of course felt even more keenly when the city was finally captured: the length of both sieges meant that on neither occasion was the Persian king inclined to restrain his soldiers from random slaughter. Civilians who survived were carried off for resettlement in Persia (cf. Lieu 1986, Kettenhofen 1994)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_317_IrSasn1]
♠ Extermination ♣ present ♥ "The Sassanid emperors' religious zeal fueled upheavals and ethnoreligious disputes in the empire and caused genocide and deportation of many tribes and ethnic groups."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_318_IrSasn1]
♠ Ethnocide ♣ present ♥ Zoroastrian priest Kerdir "began the persecution of the religious minorities in the empire, such as the Jews, Christians, Manichaeans, Mandeans, and Buddhists. ... Mani ... was imprisoned and put to death in 276 with the blessing (and to the relief) of Kerdir."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_319_IrSasn1] Bahram V (420-438 CE) and Yazdgird II (438-457 CE) persecuted Christians. "Bahram V continued and intensified the persecution of Yazdagird's last days." Forced conversions. Property confiscated. Churches destroyed.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_320_IrSasn1] Some persecution of Jews during reign of Yazdgird II (438-457 CE) "particularly in Isfahan which was the centre of the Jewish community, but it was of short duration. Nevertheless some Jews subsequently emigrated to Arabia and India. [...] There were sizeable Jewish communities in the towns of Mesopotamia and Iran: that in Iran was self-governing and was generally not persecuted by the Sasanians."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_321_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Archaeological proxies:
♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The following quote refers to the site of Roman-held Dura-Europos, in Syria, besieged and destroyed by the Sasanians around 256 CE. "Here, lying in a Roman countermine intended to disrupt Sasanian attempt to undermine or sap the city defenses, du Mesnil found a tightly packed tangle of up to 20 bodies he identified as Roman soldiers, still with their equipment and their last pay in their purses, and nearby another armored skeleton interpreted as one of the Sasanian attackers."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_322_IrSasn1] However, there is no evidence for trauma in these skeletons, which, among other things, led the author of the article to infer that they had been killed by chemical attack.
♠ Mass burials ♣ present ♥ The following quote refers to the site of Roman-held Dura-Europos, in Syria, besieged and destroyed by the Sasanians around 256 CE. "Here, lying in a Roman countermine intended to disrupt Sasanian attempt to undermine or sap the city defenses, du Mesnil found a tightly packed tangle of up to 20 bodies he identified as Roman soldiers, still with their equipment and their last pay in their purses, and nearby another armored skeleton interpreted as one of the Sasanian attackers. [...] [I]t seems that the slaughtered soldiers may not, as du Mesnil believed, have fallento steel weapons or to a purposely set fire intended to destroy the mine in which they were found. Rather, the evidence suggests they were already dead or dying before they came to lie where they were discovered and that they had been killed or incapacitated as a result of deliberate gassing. It is proposed that these are the first archaeologically attested victims of what we today call chemical warfare."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_323_IrSasn1]
♠ Settlement destruction ♣ present ♥ "As noted, [Zeugma] was destroyed in the mid 3rd century AD, and this effectively meant the end of Zeugma’s prosperity (Abadie–Reynal 2000, 8; Bașgelen 2000, 10-19; Thebault 2002; 40). It was, however, precisely because of the Sassanid destruction layer securely sealing the archaeological deposit, that the wall paintings of 3rd century Zeugma, and indeed, the architectural remains of the city, are as well preserved as they are."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_324_IrSasn1]
♠ Pictorial evidence of warfare ♣ present ♥ "Sapur I disseminated the image of his victory over Valerian through less monumental, yet no less brilliant facets of Sasanian visual culture. Exhibiting a remarkable fusion of Roman and Sasanian artistic features, a gem now in Paris depicts an equestrian battle between Sapur I and Valerian".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_325_IrSasn1]

List of Wars[edit]

Sasanian-Parthian (205 CE-229 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Sasanian-Roman (231 CE-233 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Sasanian-Roman (235 CE-240 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Third Sasanian-Roman (243 CE-244 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Fourth Sasanian-Roman (252 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Fifth Sasanian-Roman (260 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Hormizd's Rebellion (283 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]note 59 11-12[MAJIDBENAM_BR]This was not the only problem that Wahram II had as we hear of religious strife as well, namely in the province of Khuzistan led by a certain mowbed who held power there for some time.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Sixth Sasanian-Roman (283 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Seventh Sasanian-Roman (296 CE-298 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Sasanian-Arabian (325 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Eighth Sasanian-Roman (337 CE-350 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Huns?[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Sasanian-Kidarite (350 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Siege of Amida (359 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Ninth Sasanian-Roman (361 CE-363 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR][[]] 23 when?[MAJIDBENAM_BR]By all accounts Wahram (421-438 CE) was a successful ruler; [...] He defeated the Hephthalites, another tribe in the east, killing their king and stopping their encroachment on the eastern borders of the empire.[MAJIDBENAM_BR][[]] 23[MAJIDBENAM_BR]In the early years of the rule of Yazdgerd II (438-457 CE), the focus shifted to the east and battling what the sources call the Kushans, probably the Huns. Yazdgerd was stationed in Khurasan for some time until he was able to secure the eastern flank of the empire, and Bactria came under the control of the Sasanians. He then moved towards Armenia and Albania, as the defense of the Caucasus from the Huns moving westward was imperative, a campaign which also involved the Romans.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]First Armenian Uprising (451 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR][[]] 24-25[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The two sons of Yazdgerd II, Hormizd III (457-459 CE) and Peroz (459-484 CE) ruled consecutively, although the latter deposed the former in a power struggle. Peroz fled to the east to Khurasan and with an army probably consisting of Kidarites or Hephthalites regained the throne. Meantime, while Hormizd III may have crowned himself, we hear that their mother, Denag was governing the capital or parts of the capital. During this confusion Albania gained independence and theeastern boarders of the empire were laid open to Hephthalite attack. When Peroz came to the throne, he pacified Albania, but allowed the Armenians to practice Christianity and made an agreement with the eastern Roman Empire to cooperate in defending the Caucus passes.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Sasanian-Hephthalite (469 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Second Armenian Uprising (482 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Third Sasanian-Hephthalite (484 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).
♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥
♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ "No Roz is the most joyous and beautiful of the Zoroastrian feasts, a spring festival invested with especial religious significance. Religiously it celebrates the creation of fire, which informs all the other six creations, being as it were their spirit or life-force" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_326_IrSasn1]. It is notoriously difficult to reconstruct the history of Zoroastrian practices [MAJIDBENAM_REF_327_IrSasn1], but there are records regarding Sasanian kings and their role in Nowruz, as well as their occasional calendrical reforms, which affected the exact time when Nowruz, specifically, was celebrated [MAJIDBENAM_REF_328_IrSasn1]. It should be noted that there were at least two other very popular Zoroastrian festivals in ancient Iran, Mehragan and Tiragan, but Nowruz was probably somewhat more important, as suggested both by the fact that it was one of the seven annual feasts with mandatory participation [MAJIDBENAM_REF_329_IrSasn1], and the fact that it was the only major Zoroastrian festival to survive the spread of Islam in the region [MAJIDBENAM_REF_330_IrSasn1].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [MAJIDBENAM_REF_331_IrSasn1].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [MAJIDBENAM_REF_332_IrSasn1].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [MAJIDBENAM_REF_333_IrSasn1]. Thomas Cressy: As the ritual is described as having 'mandatory participation', it seems there is no evidence of passive spectators. Coded as 'not applicable' instead of 'yearly'

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Yasna ♥ The Yasna ritual is the central ritual of Zoroastrianism [MAJIDBENAM_REF_374_IrSasn1]. The fact that the first sacred Zoroastrian texts were compiled during Sasanian times, including the text meant to be recited during the Yasna ritual itself, also known as Yasna, is probably sufficient proof that the ritual was performed in those times. Very briefly, the ritual consisted of the sacrifice of an animal in a specially designated place, as well as the recitation of the sacred text from which the Yasna takes its name.
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_375_IrSasn1]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_376_IrSasn1]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ daily ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_377_IrSasn1]
♠ Duration ♣ [2-3] ♥ hours. two and a half hours The liturgical text from which the Yasna takes its name is recited in full during the ritual, which means that, in modern times, the ritual lasts about two and a half hours [MAJIDBENAM_REF_378_IrSasn1]. This was probably about the same in ancient times.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ 2 ♥ It seems likely that only two priests presided, though it may have been eight for particularly important occasions [MAJIDBENAM_REF_379_IrSasn1]. No information is available regarding the number of laypeople present at the ritual. "In the Avesta, several types of priests are listed, but according to the manuscripts, only two were involved in the yasna sacrifice, the principal, or libating, priest (zaotar) and his assistant."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_380_IrSasn1] "There is no specific term for the assistant (he is called raspi, from unattestd Avestan rathwiya, 'the original one in charge of the ratus'), and he speaks only to answer the libating priest. It is the libating priest who conducted all the formal rituals".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_381_IrSasn1]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Nowruz ♥ "No Roz is the most joyous and beautiful of the Zoroastrian feasts, a spring festival invested with especial religious significance. Religiously it celebrates the creation of fire, which informs all the other six creations, being as it were their spirit or life-force" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_519_IrSasn1]. It is notoriously difficult to reconstruct the history of Zoroastrian practices [MAJIDBENAM_REF_520_IrSasn1], but there are records regarding Sasanian kings and their role in Nowruz, as well as their occasional calendrical reforms, which affected the exact time when Nowruz, specifically, was celebrated [MAJIDBENAM_REF_521_IrSasn1]. It should be noted that there were at least two other very popular Zoroastrian festivals in ancient Iran, Mehragan and Tiragan, but Nowruz was probably somewhat more important, as suggested both by the fact that it was one of the seven annual feasts with mandatory participation [MAJIDBENAM_REF_522_IrSasn1], and the fact that it was the only major Zoroastrian festival to survive the spread of Islam in the region [MAJIDBENAM_REF_523_IrSasn1].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [MAJIDBENAM_REF_524_IrSasn1].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ yearly ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [MAJIDBENAM_REF_525_IrSasn1].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ No Roz was celebrated with the coming of spring [MAJIDBENAM_REF_526_IrSasn1]. Thomas Cressy: As the ritual is described as having 'mandatory participation', it seems there is no evidence of passive spectators. Coded as 'not applicable' instead of 'yearly'

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Zoroastrian mortuary ritual ♥ The Zoroastrian ritual of exposing cadavers to the elements, and subsequently to gather the bones and deposit them in ossuaries, is believed to have become "more or less obligatory" under Sasanid rule [MAJIDBENAM_REF_567_IrSasn1]. Indeed, the most detailed ancient account of this practice dates to Sasanid times: sixth-century CE Byzantine historian Agathias wrote "this is the Persian funeral practice: the flesh is removed in this way and the sposed bones rot, scattered at random all over the plain. They consider it irreligious to place the dead in a tomb or container, or even to bury them in the ground" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_568_IrSasn1]. Sacred Zoroastrian texts instruct that "[t]he corpse is to be exposed naked, and must be bound to the ground or fastened to it with stones or bricks. The flesh must then be consumed swiftly by birds and dogs" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_569_IrSasn1]. Indeed, outside observers in ancient times often make specific reference to the dogs and birds to which the Iranian dead were destined [MAJIDBENAM_REF_570_IrSasn1]. This ritual is classified here as the most dysphoric collective ritual because dead bodies appear to have elicited feelings of fear and disgust in the participants: "Death being regarded as an evil brought about by Aŋra Mainyu (Ahriman), the Destructive Spirit, the corpse of a holy creature, particularly man or dog, is considered to be greatly infested by the druj Nasu, the creature of the Lie called Corpse-matter. Burial of “corpse matter” (nasa-niganih) would defile the earth (in the Sad dar, chap. 33, it is said that Spandārmad shudders when a corpse is buried) and was therefore prohibited" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_571_IrSasn1].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ weekly ♥ in terms of whole polity "daily" in terms of a particular ossuary possibly weekly? frequent Inferred from the fact that this ritual was performed every time somebody died.

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ By the fifth century the centralized bureaucracy was sophisticated enough that "the death of a king would not bring the empire down."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_603_IrSasn1] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_604_IrSasn1] "[Manuals for the administration of justice" contain "general information about the rights of specific state departments and officials"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_605_IrSasn1] The Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions contains "passages from official edicts and decrees."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_606_IrSasn1] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_607_IrSasn1] That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_608_IrSasn1]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ uncertain_absent_present ♥ By the fifth century the centralized bureaucracy was sophisticated enough that "the death of a king would not bring the empire down."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_609_IrSasn1] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_610_IrSasn1] "[Manuals for the administration of justice" contain "general information about the rights of specific state departments and officials"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_611_IrSasn1] The Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions contains "passages from official edicts and decrees."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_612_IrSasn1] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_613_IrSasn1] That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_614_IrSasn1] "The powerful Zoroastrian church and administrative manuals that contain information on the 'rights of specific state departments and officials' suggest that the king could not always issue edicts and decrees how he wished and that lawmaking responsibilities were distributed and under the influence most likely of the chief Zoroastrian officials. Whether this was merely the situation de facto or whether it was also considered legal might depend on the personality of the king. The king could "replace" the mowbedan mowbed "and pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_615_IrSasn1]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ absent: 205-274 CE; present: 275-293 CE; inferred present: 294-325 CE; inferred absent: 326-378 CE; inferred present: 379-409 CE; inferred absent: 410-420 CE ♥ absent: 205-274 CE: "Executive power is not separate from judiciary. e.g. Papak (208-222 CE) was a Zoroastrian priest. present: 275-293 CE: Zoroastrian priests after Bahram II had de jure authority. Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_616_IrSasn1] The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[MAJIDBENAM_REF_617_IrSasn1] whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_618_IrSasn1] is unknown. inferred present: 294-325 CE: presumably the situation is the same under kings Narseh (293-303 CE) and Hormizd II (303-309 CE) and during the infancy of Shapur II when "the court and the Zoroastrian priests ran an empire that was secure and stable enough structurally and administratively to survive without a strong monarchy"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_619_IrSasn1]. inferred absent: 326-378 CE: Under Shapur II, power of the nobility and priests increased substantially.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_620_IrSasn1] An inscription relates that Ardashar II (379-383 CE) purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_621_IrSasn1] inferred present: 379-409 CE: The sophisticated, centralised bureaucracy was now "under the control of the priests" and its chief priest, with Kingship relegated to the status of a secular institution.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_622_IrSasn1] However, the kings that followed "all met a violent end."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_623_IrSasn1] "that appears to mean up to 420 CE: Shapur III, Wahram IV, Yazdgird I, Shapur IV, Khosrau the Usurper (?). inferred absent: 410-420 CE the 'secular' kings became powerful enough to challenge the priests. Under Yazdgerd I (399-420 CE) Christianity was officially recognized[MAJIDBENAM_REF_624_IrSasn1] which is not something the Zoroastrian hierarchy would have done. It has been noted that the king could "replace" the mowbedan mowbed "and pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_625_IrSasn1] and this could refer to this violent time period when the executive claimed the highest judicial authority (the violence suggests Zoroastrian establishment disputed this). The reign of kings who meet a violent end appears to finish after the Usurper interlude with the reign of Bahram V (420-438 CE). Bahram V and Yazdgird II are noted for persecution of Christians and Jews. At this time we could conclude that the Zoroastrian priests, not the king, were again the de jure legal authority."[MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Right to petition executive ♣ inferred absent ♥ Sasanid society had four classes: warriors, scribes, priests, and commoners. The warriors (Arteshtaran) were an hereditary elite.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_626_IrSasn1] Seven aristocratic families dominated the military and government leadership positions. All except the Sassans were Parthian in origin. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_627_IrSasn1] The warriors (Arteshtaran) were an hereditary elite.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_628_IrSasn1] Some of the powerful families at the top of the Sasanid state "held hereditary offices, both civil and military".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_629_IrSasn1] Formal "charters of rank (gah-namag)" showed an individual's precise status in relation to society and the king.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_630_IrSasn1] Nobles were also graded among themselves.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_631_IrSasn1] The sahryaran (local dynasties), waspuhragan (princes), wuzurgan (heads of aristocratic families), azadan (other noblement). Membership of one of these noble grades was a prerequisite for "access to any state or court office of importance."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_632_IrSasn1] This was a very rigid caste society with a strong religious hierarchy so it is probably unlikely that a peasant would be able to petition the executive to change the law.
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ present ♥ "[Manuals for the administration of justice" contain "general information about the rights of specific state departments and officials".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_633_IrSasn1] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_634_IrSasn1] That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_635_IrSasn1]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ present ♥ Under Bahram II (274-293 CE) "the Sasanian kings lost much of their religious power as caretakers of the Anahid fire temple to Kerdir, who became the judge of the whole empire. ... from this point on, the priests acted as judges throughout the empire, and court cases were probably based on Zoroastrian law except when members of other religious minorities had disputes with each other."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_636_IrSasn1] The highest legal official was the mowbedan mowbed, the top religious leader within the Zoroastrian church[MAJIDBENAM_REF_637_IrSasn1] whose precise relationship with the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State, the head of the state judges)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_638_IrSasn1] is unknown. The king could "replace" the mowbedan mowbed "and pass judgement in criminal cases, as we may conclude from the Acts of the Christian Martyrs (see Wiessner 1967)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_639_IrSasn1][MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ present ♥ During the infancy of Shapur II (309-379 CE) "the court and the Zoroastrian priests ran an empire that was secure and stable enough structurally and administratively to survive without a strong monarchy. This scenario also signaled to the courtiers and the nobility that the empire could be managed without a powerful king."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_640_IrSasn1] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_641_IrSasn1] "[Manuals for the administration of justice" contain "general information about the rights of specific state departments and officials"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_642_IrSasn1] The Book of a Thousand Judicial Decisions contains "passages from official edicts and decrees."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_643_IrSasn1] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_644_IrSasn1] That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_645_IrSasn1] Sasanids agreed to be the guardian of Theodosius II, the son of and successor to the East Roman Emperor Arcadius (383-408 CE). From this time "guardianship of the hiers to the throne of the respective empires" was sometimes requested and granted on both sides. The fact this helped to secure the succession possibly indicates "each were more fearful of internal opposition than of each other."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_646_IrSasn1] Balash (484-488 CE) was deposed by nobility and priests.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_647_IrSasn1] An inscription relates that Ardashar II (379-383 CE) purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_648_IrSasn1] Fifth century kings were "generally weak, and the nobility and the Zoroastrian priests were able to conduct their activities at the expense of the royal power."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_649_IrSasn1] The first reign of Kavad I (488-496 CE) was ended by "dissatisfied nobility and priests" who had him imprisoned.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_650_IrSasn1] Head of "seven great families", with lineages from the Achaemenid period, who were feudal rulers. At least before the sixth century fiscal reforms of Khusrau I the peasants paid taxes to them in addition to the Sasanid rulers, and they held both civil and military office.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_651_IrSasn1]

♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ In Zoroastrian tradition after death bones (asti) and (breath-)soul (urwan) are 'wrenched apart'. The breath-soul is judged at the Ford of the Accountant "where rewards or punishments are meted out according to the total account of the persons good and evil thoughts, words, and deeds." The souls of the rightuous go to a good place - perhaps a House of Songs - while the wicked souls are taken to a bad place e.g. 'Endless Darkness'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_707_IrSasn1] The first Sasanian ruler in Istakhr, Pabag, may have succeeded in his revolt having persuaded local warriors "who were devoted to the Zoroastrian goddess. Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥Administrative capital was at Ctesiphon.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_730_IrSasn1] Increase in bureaucracy under Shapur I.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_731_IrSasn1] By the fifth century the centralized bureaucracy was sophisticated enough that "the death of a king would not bring the empire down."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_732_IrSasn1] "Sassanid administration was headed by a Grand Vizier, who was in charge of political and diplomatic affairs. On occasion he commanded the army in the field. He also headed the divans (ministries), which were directed by secretaries expert in their various fields."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_733_IrSasn1] Secretaries of a divan (ministry)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_734_IrSasn1] Scribes were called dirbirs.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_735_IrSasn1] Treasurers called ganzwars.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_736_IrSasn1] There was a central archives: "Important events of the reign of each of the Sasanian kings were written down and preserved in the imperial archives, a practice that probably dates from the very beginning of Sasanian rule."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_737_IrSasn1] The city of Bishapur, founded by Shapur I c262 CE, had a Greek plan,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_738_IrSasn1] which perhaps shows skills and resources inherited from Greek administrators who were common under Parthian rule. The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_739_IrSasn1] (That society was "based on descent groups which were the most influential political, religious, and economic units."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_740_IrSasn1]).

There also was a top state judge official the sahr dadwaran dadwar (the judge of the judges of the State) who may have been responsible for the dadwar state judges although his exact responsibilites are unknown.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Source of Support of state-level officials

♠ Other ♣ ♥Pathologies
♠ Corruption ♣ widespread ♥ according to Ammianus Marcellinus (XXIII, 6.75-80) the Sassanid elite claimed "the power of life and death over slaves and commons."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_765_IrSasn1] An inscription relates that Ardashar II (379-383 CE) purged "the great men and holders of authority to reduce their power."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_766_IrSasn1] The king often recruited administrators to the bureaucracy from outside the dominant families. This new class known as "'the grandees and nobles' had the function, in effect, of checking the power of the great families from encroaching. This class included the high state officers, ministers, heads of the administration, and royal officials. Its growth and development introduced a new element into society".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_767_IrSasn1] We might also conclude from this behaviour that the bureaucrats within the central administration, coming from the outside and dependent on the king, would have been more loyal to the king. On the other hand, the fact the king tried hard to do this suggests there may have been problems with officials recruited from the dominant families.
